stimulating\n\nfrom\n380 to\nthe photorecep-\n\ntors of the eye thereby\nproducing\na sensation\ncalled vislon.\nThe physical\nphenomenon\nof light may\nbe viewed\nin two different ways: as cornea,\nthen through\nthe lens and vitreous\nhumor\nonto the retina.\nData\non the spectral\ntransmlsslon\nof the ocular\nmedia\nare available\n(63).\nThe\n\nrods\n\nand\n\ncones\n\nof the\n\nretina\n\ntransduce\n\nthe light into neuro-electrical\n\nphenomena.\nThe neural\nelements\nof the retina are gathered\ninto the optic\nnerve\nat the blind spot and pass by discrete\npathways\nto the highest\nvisual\ncenter\nin the braln in the occipital cortex.\nThe human\nvisual system\nis a very versatile\none, with ample\ncapabilities\nof adaptation\nto a variety of Figure\nZ-18 shows\nthe maximum\nvisual angle for a\npoint source\nat different background\nluminances.\nNote that the angle increases\nwith decreasing\nbackground\nluminance.\nIn the debriefings\nof ";.\n\nThere are several ways in which\nvisual acuity has been defined and\nmeasured, each of which has significance for detection and recognition of\ndetail. These are defined in Figure\n2-15.\n\n/\n\nThe luminance contrast between\ntarget and background determines the\nminimum visual angle which can be\ndetected. Illuminant\n\nThe color of the illuminant\ncan be controlled\neither\nby filters between\nthe source\nand the observer\'s\neyes.\nthe same\neffect.\nColored\nilluminants\ncause the loss or\n\nat the light source\nBoth methods\ngive\nreduction\nof color\n\nor\n\ncontrast\nand the distortion\nof the normal\nluminance\nrelations.\nObjects\nof the\nsame\ncolor as the illuminant\nwill be relatively increased\nin luminance\nand\nmay\nbecome\ninvisible against\na light background.\nObjects\nof complementary\ncolor will be darkened\nand may\nbe invisible against\na dark background.\nThis\neffect can be used to advantage\nin some\nhighly specific applications.\nIn most\ncases\nthis distortion\nof normal\nbrightness\nand color relationships\nis a serious\nhandicap,\nand greatly reduces\nthe total information\nwhich\ncan be resolved\nby\nthe eye.\nExperimental\nfindings concerning\nvisual acuity and color of the illuminant have been somewhat\ncontradictory\n(26, 146 I. When\nthere is a large\nluminance\ncontrast\nbetween\ntest object and background,\nvisual acuity varies\nonly slightly with wavelength\nand is generally\nbest near the middle\nof the\nvlsible spectrum,\nif all test objects are of equal luminance.\nReducing\nthe\nluminance\ncontrast\nbetween\ntest object and background\ndegrades\nacuity\nsimilarly\nat all wavelengths\nso there is little, if any, interaction\nbetween\nwavelength\nand contrast\n(89).\nVisual acuity with red and blue backgrounds\nof different lumlnance\nis shown\nin Figure\n2-29.\n(See also chromatic\naberration.\n)\n\n2-28\n\nL\n\n-\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n-\n\n00\n\n2O\n\n1 500 000\n300 000\n150000\n120 000\n90 000\n\n10\n\n5\n4\n3\n\n6O0O0\n%\n\n45000\n\nLU\nL_\nZ\n\n30 OOC\n\n2\ncO\n\nO_\n\n..-j\n\n27 000\n240OO\n\nL9\n\no\n.J\no\no\n\n0.4\n0.3\n0.2\n\n18000\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 -\n\n.\n\nz\no\n\n21000\n\ntlJ\n\nr\n\n<\noc\n\n0.1\n\n_E\n0.05\n0.04\n0.03\n\n15000\n\n0.02\n\n12000\n\n0.01\n\nSIGHTING\n\nRANGE - considered\n\n(435).\nColor\n\nSpecification\n\nColor\nmay\nbe considered\nas having\nthree psychological\ncomponents:\nsaturation,\nand brightness.\nMost\nsystems\nof color specification\nmake\nthese three concepts\nin one form\nor another.\n\nuse\n\nhue,\nof\n\nHue is the aspect\nof color commonly\ndenoted\nby such names\nas red,\nyellow,\ngreen,\nblue, orange,\nand many\nothers.\nThe most\nclosely\nrelated\nphysical\nproperty\nof light is wavelength\n-- though\nthe hue purple\ndoes not\ncorrespond\nto any wavelength\nin the spectrum.\nSaturation\nis defined as the degree\nto which\na sensation\nof hue differs\nfrom\na gray of the same\nbrightness.\nColors\nthat are i00 percent\nsaturated\nare called spectrum\ncolors.\nWhen\nwhite light is added to a spectrum\ncolor,\nthe spectrum\ncolor decreases\nin saturation.\nFor example,\na spectrum\nred\nbecomes\nmore\nor less pink when\nit is mixed\nwith white light; it is still red\nin hue, but its saturation\nhas decreased.\nThe\nreaching\n\nZ-3Z\n\nsensation\nof brightness\nthe eye from\nan object\n\nis related to the amount\nor light source.\nOther\n\nof luminous\nthings being\n\nflux\nequal,\n\n_;i \xc2\xb8 will be confused.\nAlso,\nno attempt\nshould\nbe made\nto include\nwhite or yellow\nin conjunction\nwith the three recommended\ncolors\nbecause\nthe color-deficient\nindividuals\nmay\nconfuse\nred with yellow\nand green with white.\nA color-coding\nscheme\nfor indicator\nlights on\ning to the identification\ncolors\nlisted in MIL-C-Z5050\nApollo\nsystem\nare noted in Figure\n2-3Zc\n( 3 mL\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFigure\nInstantaneous\n(Adapted\n\nfrom\n\n.\n\n.\n\n.,\n\n2-44\n\nThreshold\nNutting\n\nfor\n\nLight\n\n(322))\n\ncan see a I0 minute square target about one hundredth as bright immediately\nafter the pre-adapting\nfield is turned off. If the observer in question were\nexposed to a field luminance of 1000 mL but the target luminance was 0.0001\nmE,\nthe predicted luminance threshold in Figure Z-4Za indicates he must\nwait about I0 minutes after entering a dark room before he can see the target\nlight. Figure 2-44 is for simple light detection and does not permit a prediction of instantaneous visual acuity threshold, which requires discrimination\nof form.\nDark adaptation data pertinent to cathode ray tube (CIRT) displays\nare seen in Figures 2-67 to 2-69.\nTraining techniques n-ay aid in optimizing\nlunar surface operations (286, 417 ).\n\n2-50\n\n.\n\nnight vision, especially\n\nduring\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n-.\n\n-.\n\nGlare\n\nand\n\nFlash\n\nBlindness\n\nPhenomena\n\nGlare\nis defined\nas any degree\nof light falling upon the retina in excess\nof that which\nenables\none to see clearly; that is, any excess\nof light which\nhinders\ninstead of helps vision.\nGlare can be furthe\'r differentiated\ninto:\n(I) Veiling glare: created\nby light uniformly\nsuperimposed\non\nthe retinal image\nwhich\nreduces\ncontrast\nand, therefore,\nvi s ibility\n(2) Dazzling\nglare: adventitious\nlight scattered\nin the ocular\nmedia\nso as not to form\npart of the retinal image\n(3) Scotomatic\nor blinding\nglare (flash blindness):\nproduced\nby light of sufficient intensity to reduce\nthe sensitivity\nof\nthe retina.\n\n-/\n\n4-\n\nAlthough\nall three types of glare are present\nin the case of high-intensity\nlight, the effects of the first two are primarily\nevident only when\nthe source\nis present.\nThe third type, scotomatic\nor blinding glare,\nis especially\nsignificant in flash blindness\nwhere\nit produces\nsymptoms\n(afterimages)\nwhich\npersist\nlong after the light itself has vanished.\nThe afterimage\nis a prolongation of the physiological\nprocesses\nwhich\nproduced\nthe original\nsensation\nresponse\nafter cessation\nof stimulation.\nGlare\nRegardless\nof whether\nthe glare source\nis direct or indirect\n(reflected or\nspecular),\nit can cause discomfort,\nor it can affect the visual performance,\nor\nit can do both.\nThe visual discomfort\nor annoyance\nfrom\nglare is a common\nwell-understood\nexperience\nand has been confirmed\nby many\nexperiments.\nIn\nconnection\nwith certain experimental\nstudies,\nit has been found that people\nsometimes\nbecome\nmore\nphysically\ntense and restless\nunder\nglare conditions\nthan under\nnonglare\nconditions.\n\n.\n\n-,j\n\nIn general,\nvisual acuity is at a maximum\nwhen\nthe eyes are adapted\nto\nthe brightness\nlevel of the target and background.\nBut acuity is reduced\nwhen\nthe target and background\nare at a lower brightness\nthan the greater\nsurround.\nThe curve\nin Figure\nZ-45 shows\nthe effect of surround\nbrightness\nboth darker\nand lighter than the target and the immediate\nbackground\n_78).\nAcuity\nis best\nwhen\nthe surround\nis a little darker\nthan the target.\nThus,\nit is suggested\nthat\nfor best acuity, targets\nshould not be in a shadow\nor near a large area of\nmuch\nhigher\nbrightness.\nRecent\nreviews\nof surround\nbrightness\nand size on\nvisual performance\nare available\n(223,224).\nVisual\nacuity is best when\nthe eyes have not been exposed\nto high levels\nof brightness.\nAs a general\nrule if visual displays\nhave to be read by people\nwho have just been exposed\nto high levels of brightness\n(the open sea, clouds,\ndesert,\nor snow),\nthe level of brightness\nof the displays\nshould be higher\nthan would\nnormally\nbe necessary\n- they should be at least 0.01 as bright as\nthe pre-exposure\nfield (see Figure\n2-44).\nFrom\n\na practical\n\nstandpoint,\n\ndata\n\nare\n\navailable\n\nwhich\n\nspecifying\nincreased\ndisplay\ncontrast\nrequirements\nwhen\nthe display is substantially\nbrighter\nthan the background\n\nprovide\n\na basis\n\nfor\n\nthe area surrounding\nwithin the display.\n2-51\n\nik\xc2\xb8 \xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\n\xe2\x80\xa2i\n\n0.56\nBrightness of\ntosk oreo BS/BB\n\n=\n\nl)\n\nbrightness\nbrightness\n\nWhile\nthe effect of glare on visual performance\ncan be of serious\nconsequence\nby itself, the visual discomfort\nbrought\nabout by glare can also be a\nmatter\nof some\nconcern.\nThough\nthe cause\nis physical,\nthe discomfort\nbrought\nabout by it is often of a subjective\nnature.\nThe evaluation\nof discomfort,\nthen, must\nmake\nuse of subjective\nresponses\nas criteria\n( 13,189 ,\n190,272\n\n).\n\nInvolved\nin such procedures\nis the concept\nof the "borderline\nbetween\ncomfort\nand discomfort"\n(BCD)\n(272).\nThe variables\nwhich\ngovern\nwhether\na\nvisual environment\nis comfortable\nor not include two groups.\nFirst are\nthose that are basic to the situation,\nsuch as brightness\nof the (glare) source\nor luminous\narea,\nits visual size, and the brightness\nof the surrounding\nfield\nor area and adaptation\nbrightness.\nSecond\nare certain factors that have a\nmodifying\n.effect, including\nthe position of the source\nin the visual field, the\nnumber\nof sources\nin the visual field, and their configuration\nand arrangement.\nIndices of discomfort\nrelated to these variables\nare available\n( 155, 191).\nThe effect of angle of the glare source\nrelative to the visual axis on\nvisual performance\nhas received\nstudy {273,278\n}. source\nwhen\nF varies\nfrom\n1.0 to 0.01 footlamberts\n\nand discomfort\nis\n(F) and the size (Q)\nand Q from\n0.0011\n\nto 0.000001\nsteradian.\nThe experimental\nresults show\nthat the BCD\nbrightness in these ranges\nof adaptation\nbrightness\nand source\nsize does not vary\nlinearly as would be indicated\nby extrapolations\nof several\ninvestigations\n(Figure\nZ-46c).\nAs the glare source\nbecomes\nsmaller\nthan 10-5 steradian,\nthe BCD\nbrightness\nof the glare source\nbecomes\ngreater\nat an increasing\nrate, indicating\nthat very high brightnesses\nmay\nnot be non-scattering\nvacuum\nthe relative effects of glare in distorting the usual\ncues for depth and size, perception\nand rate of closure\nmay\nbe altered\n(367, 410).\nThis will require\nfurther\nstudy.\nIrradiation\n\nPhenomena\n\nAn observer\nattempting\nto measure\nthe boundary\nbetween\na bright area\nand a darker\narea will perceive\nthe boundary\nto lie toward\nthe darker\narea.\nAstronomers\nrefer to this effect as "irradiation"\nand define it in operational\nterms\nas the spreading\nof a bright image\non the retina of the eye making\nthe\ndiameter\nof any bright object to appear\nto be larger\nthan it really is (30 , 415\nThe magnitude\nof the effect of irradiation\non astronomical\nangular\nmeasurements\nwith optical instruments\nvaries\nwith the luminance\nof the\nbright limb,\nthe contrast\nof the bright limb against the background,\nthe\noptical system\nused,\nthe dark adaptation\nof the observer,\nand the individual\nobserver.\nIn astronomy,\ncorrections\nfor irradiation\nare empirical\nand\nare\nderived\nfrom\nperformance\nrecords\nof experienced\nobservers.\nFor example,\nthe Nautical\nAlmanac\nuses an irradiation\ncorrection\nof l.Z minutes\nof arc\nfor measurements\nsextant.\n\n2-56\n\nof the altitude\n\nof the upper\n\nlimb\n\nof the Sun\n\nwith\n\nthe marine\n\n).\n\n\'\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSeveral investigations have been performed which are related to the\nproblem of visually sighting a space vehicle near the sun (i04, I05). Measures of the minimal angle of resolution (MAR) for two small self-luminous\nobjects against an unilluminated background have also been studied and have\nindicated that MAR tends to increase as a functionof object luminance. For\ntwo point sources against different background illuminations, the MAR was\nfound to depend entirely upon contrast and not upon the absolute value of\nbackground lumlnance (325). The MAR increases linearly as a function of\nthe logarithm of the luminance ratio from an Is/I b of i00 to at least 5 x 104.\nThe MAR associated with the highest contrast ratio in this study was found\nto be 4.0 minutes of arc for glare-source luminance of 4000 ml at a background of 0. 07 millilamberts.\nAt luminance ratios of less than I00, the MAR\nis constant at about i. 8 minutes of arc.\nOther of the visual variables involved in celestial navigation will be\ngreatly affected if the observer looks at or near the sun or other intense\nsource. Perception of a movlng point source in close proximity to a source\nof high luminance and the correspondence between physical form and perceived shape are factors to be considered in such a task. The variables\ninclude (1) glare-source shape, (Z) glare-source intensity, (3) point-source\ndirectlon of movement in eight frontal-plane meridians, and (4) point-source\ndirection within each meridian. In a recent study of this important problem,\nfive highly trained observers viewed a stimulus configuration through an\nartificial pupil which provided a 10-1/Z \xc2\xb0 field of view (194). The moving\n"star" was used as a test spot to determine the characteristics of the luminous field gradient produced by the glare source of only 4250 ft Z. It was\nfound that the distance, in visual angle, from the perceived edge of a glare\nsource at which a star disappears (or reappears) is directly related to the\nluminance of the glare source. This appears to be a curvilinear function\nwhich accelerates rapidly at about 1000 ft L and again begins to decelerate\nat about 4000 ft Z as shown in Figure 2-47.\n\n.\n\n..r\n\n_\n\n-\n\n-\n\n\xc2\xb0\n\n-r -\n\n"\n\n-\n\n...."\n\n.i\'i\n\nL\n\nThis finding is somewhat greater than those obtained for two point\nsources (325). The star disappears and reappears at different apparent distances from the edge of the glare source, depending upon what kind of edge\ngeometry exists. An example of a straight edge can be found on the sides of\nthe square and triangular mirrors.\nThe edge of the circular mirror provides\nan example of a curved edge. The star disappears and reappears farther\nfrom the curved edges than it does for straight line edges under equivalent\nluminance conditions. Figure 2-48 presents these data.\nThe irradiation phenomenon results in distortion of shapes of the object\n(194,367).\nThe apparent size of the glare source increases as a function\nof its lumlnance. This effect can also be seen in Figure 2-49. The response\nvariance tends to be larger under the higher luminance conditions than under\nlower luminance conditions; however, variance does not appear to be significantly affected by either the shape of the glare source or the meridian of\ntravel of a test star (194).\nIt can be concluded that as a result of the irradiation phenomenon, (I)\nnavigational sightings should be confined to a vision envelope which does not\ninclude any extremely bright sources aimed in the direction of the eye.\n2-57\n\nr\n\nk\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n-\'\n\n.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nk\n\nJ\ni\n\n2"30 \' .....\n\n:\n\nr\n\n.....\n\n\'.-\n\n-\n\n-\n\nlight is the primary\nsensation\nof light followed\nby a serles of positive and\nnegative\nafterimages.\nWith moderate\nlight intensities,\nafterimages\nare not\nnoticed\nbecause\nof the complex\naction of successive\nstimulation\nand continuous\nmovement\nof the eye.\nHowever,\nif the original stimulation\nis of sufficient\nduration\nand intensity, the sensation\nwill persist\nwith an intensity adequate\nto reduce\nor entirely obliterate\nfoveal perception\nuntil the effect is dissipated.\nThis is the primary\nfactor in flashblindness.\nPapers\nfrom\na recent\nsymposium\non the loss of vision from\nhigh intensity light are now available\n{ 2 ).\nThe amount\nof visually effective light entering\nthe eye, because\nit is an\nindication\nof the amount\nof photopigment\nbleached,\nseems\nto be the essential\nfactor in the formation\nof the afterimage\n(63 , 95\n, 297).\nA useful unit for\nspecifying\nthe amount\nof visually effective light is the troland-second,\nbecause\nit includes\nthe area of the eye pupil and has the dimensions\nof retinal illuminance assuming\nperfect\nocular\ntransmission\n(see Table\nZ-Z).\nThe data may\nalso be presented\nas cal/cm\n2 at the retina.\nA glossary\nof radiometric\nand\nphotometric\nconcepts\nused in flash blindness\nand retinal burn\nresearch\nis\navailable\n(19).\nAttempts\n\nhave\n\nbeen\n\nmade\n\nto relate\n\nthe intensity\n\nof light flashes\n\nto the\n\nalterations\nin sensitivity of the d#rk-adapted\neye.\nAt relatively low illuminances\nof less than 50 lumens/ft\n, there is found no alteration\nin the course\nof dark adaptation\nmomentary\nlosses\nthat within certain\n\nand a general\nof sensitivity\nlimits Z x T\n\ncorrelation\nwith the reciprocity\nlaw for\n{10 , 400).\nThe reciprocity\nlaw indicates\n= IK when\nL is in units of luminance\nof the\n\ndazzle\nand T is the duration\nof the dazzle.\nAt a light source\nof moderate\nintensity, validity of the law has been confirmed\n(if0, 154, 248).\nThe law\nappears\nto hold up to 30,000\nm-L-sec.\nAbove\nthis level the effect of intensity\nfactor becomes\nrelatively\ngreater\nthan duration\n(306).\nAlso,\nfor energy\nin the\nrange\nof 3 x 107 troland-sec.,\nreciprocity\nappears\nto fall; flash durations\nof 1 millisecond\nbeing less effective than longer\nflashes\n(298).\nTheories\non\nreciprocity\nfailure have been presented\n( 458}.\nIn establishing\ncriteria for visual recovery\ntime after exposure\nto flash,\nthere is some\ndifficulty in comparing\nthe results of different investigations,\nbecause\nthe recovery\ntimes\ndepend\non the total effective integrated\nenergy\nin\nthe flash, the duration\nof the flash, the size of the critical detail in the target\nused to determine\nthe recovery,\nand the luminance\nof the target.\nThe pulse\nshapes\nmay\nalso be an important\nfactor for the very long durations.\nThe\nrecovery\ntime of visual performance\nfollowing\nhigh-intensity\nflashes is related to the decay\nof the afterimage\nbrightness\nwhich\nis a function of the\namount\nof photopigment\nbleached\nby the flash.\nThe afterimage\nreduces\nthe\nperceived\ncontrast\nof a visual display\nin the same\nmanner\nas the addition of\na uniform\nluminance\nover the display\n(297).\nFigure\n2-50 summarizes\ndata from\nseveral\nstudies on effect of flash\nenergy\nin lambert-seconds\nand recovery\ntime for perception\nof targets\nat\ntwo different levels of target atmosphere\n(121).\nThe first two phenomena\ntend to distort and blur fine image\ndetails when\nthey intervene\nbetween\nviewer\nand target,\nbut the effect will\ndiminish\nas a function of the distance\nfrom\nthe effects since the angle they\nproject\nrelative to the target will diminish.\nThe dust particles\ncan cause\nback-scattering\nof light, increasing\nbackground\nluminance\nand reducing\ncontrast,\nbut again with an effect diminishing\nwith range.\nThese\nphenomena\ncannot\nfully explain an increased\nhuman\nacuity in space,\nhowever,\nbecause\nthe limit of 1 minute\nof arc has been derived\nover the years under\nlaboratory\nconditions\nin which,\nof course,\natmospheric\neffects do not bear on the results.\nGiven\nan excellent\nlens, sensitive\nfilm and the proper\nexposure,\na camera\ncan see whatever\nlight reaches\nit, but the eye has an intrinsic threshold\nboth\nfor light energy\nand contrast\nbeyond\nwhich\nit does not respond.\nWhat\nspace\nflight suggests\nis that this threshold\nis lower than had been supposed;\nremove\ngravity and possibly\nthe eye can operate\nmore\neffectively\nas a sensor,\napproaching\nor perhaps\nreaching\nits THE\nDESIGN\nOF\nAND\nSPACE\nSUITS\n\nTHE\n\n.\n\nr\n\nThe preceding\ndata have been presented\nso as to allow prediction\nof\nvisual capabilities\non the ground\nand in space.\nThey\ncan be used for specific\nproblems\nin operational\nanalysis\nas well as design.\nThe following\nsectlon\ncovers\nthe general\ndesign\nrecommendation\nfor spacecraft\nillumination,\ninstrumentation\nand displays\nconsidering\noverall habitability\nas well as\nspecific operational\nproblems.\n.\n\nr\n\nF\n\nGeneral\n\nRecommendations\n\nfor Spacecraft\n\nIllumination\n\nExperience\nin submarines\nand naval vessels\nhas provided\nguidelines\nfor optimizing\nthe visual background\nand decor within the spacecraft.\nThe\nfollowing\nsuggestions\nare offered as a result of this and other experiences\n(5, 48, 152, Z71, 301, 389, 412, 419).\nIn the design\nof the lighting subsystem,\nit is necessary\nto consider\nthose\nfactors which\nare basic to the provision\nof artificial illumination\nn_os_\nadequate\nfor n.aintaining\ncomlortable,\nhealthful and effective functloning\nof normal\neyes.\nThese\nfactors are:\nquali_yor\ncolor of light, intensity of\nlight, and distribution\nof illumination\nand brightnesses\nwithin the environment.\nArrangement\nof these factors\nshould\ngive compartments\na more\npleasing\nappearance\nand proportions;\nall three should be combined\nto induce\nin the\noccupants\na sense\nof tranquility,\ncomfort,\nand "horniness".\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nr\n\n.\n\nThe use of color,\nas a characteristic\nof a reflecting medium,\nwill be\nconsidered\nin more\ndetail later.\nIlluminants,\nhowever,\nwhich\nvary in\nspectral\ncharacteristics,\nhave an inherent\ncolor, which\nin laboratory\nsituations, at least, affects to some\nextent the visual acuity.\nFor threshold\nseeing,\nspectral\nyellow,\nor the yellow\nlight from\nsodium\nvapor\nlamps,\nis\nsomewhat\nmore\neffective than other artificial illuminants,\nalthough\nthe\ndifference\nis not of operational\nsignificance\nand does not improve\nthe acuity\nover that from\na similar\nlevel of diffused daylight.\nIn ordinary\nsituations\n(e.g., print reading)\nthe qualities of daylight,\nmercury\narc light, tungsten\nfilament\nlight, and fluorescent\nlamps\nare all about equally effective as\nilluminants,\nalthough\nfluorescent\nlight has been criticized\nbecause\nof its\nharsh,\ncold appearance.\nThe intensity of light has to be considered\nin relation to: I) visual\nacuity;\nZ) size of object to be discriminated;\n3) speed of vision;\n4) brightness contrast;\nand\n5) efficiency of performance\n(48).\nThese\nhave been\ncovered\nin the previous\nsection.\nThese\ndata suggest\nthat visual acuity\nincreases\nrapidly up to about 5 foot-candles,\nand more\nslowly thereafter, when\nthe object to be discriminated\nis of about 3-6 minutes\nof arc.\nFor\nsmaller\nobjects,\nvision improves\nperceptibly\nup to 40 to 50 foot-candles.\nThe\ngreater\nthe brightness\ncontrast,\nthe better is the visual efficiency,\nand\nboth acuity and speed of vision continue\nto improve\nslightly up to and beyond\ni00 foot-candles.\nHowever,\nlittle is gained in acuity by increasing\nthe\nillumination\nbeyond\n25 foot-candles,\nand there is no practical\ngain at all\nwhen\nthe intensity is higher\nthan 50 foot-candles.\nFor large objects,\nZ-73\n\ni_\n\n,\n\n_\n\n,\n\n.\n\n_.\n\nsubtending\nfour minutes\nin visual discrimination\n\nof arc and above,\nwith illumination\n\nthere\nabove\n\nsmaller\nobjects,\nthere is improved\nvisual\nup to a limit of about 40-50 foot-candles.\n\nis no practical\nZ0 foot-candles.\n\ndiscrimination\n\nat higher\n\nThe data on speed of vision may\nbe summarized\nas follows:\nobjects\nsubtending\nthree minutes\nof arc or larger,\nand with good\nbetween\nobject and background,\nspeed of vision is near maximum\n15-Z0\nfoot-candles;\nb) for small\nobjects on a background\nof low\nsignificant\nup to about\nWith\n\ndecreases\nin time\n50 foot-candles.\nrespect\n\nto brightness\n\nfor seeing\n\ncontrast,\n\noccur\n\nwhen\n\nwith\n\nillumination\n\nthe contrast\n\nimprovement\nFor\nlevels\n\na) for\ncontrast\nat about\nreflectance\nintensities\n\nbetween\n\nobject\n\nand background\nis high, discrimination\nof arc sizes of three to six mlnutes\nis not significantly improved\nby illumination\nabove\nZ0 foot-candles.\nWith\nan object of l-minute\nsize, performance\nimproves\nsignificantly\nup to a\npractical\nlimit of about 50-60\nfoot-candles.\nThe greater\nthe brightness\ncontrast,\nthe better is the visual discrimination,\nalthough,\nas will be noted\nin the examination\nof light distribution,\nthe effort of seeing\nis more\nfatiguing\nwith high contrast.\nExcessive\nillumination\nwill not compensate\nfor small\nobject size or poor contrast.\nBrightness\ncontrast\ntion of illumination,\nand\n\nis also of significance\nin consideration\nof distribuin this connection\nit must\nbe distinguished\nfrom\n\nglare.\nThe effects of glare can be minimized\nby increasing\nthe brightness\ncontrast\nbetween\nthe object and its immediate\nbackground,\nand by increasing the illumination\non the visual object.\nElimination\nof the glare source,\nhowever,\nby removal,\nor by use of diffuse or indirect lighting, is superior.\nFor best overall vision,\nwith minimuro\nfatigue, the brightness\nratio\nbetween\nthe central field and\nsurround\nshould not exceed\nthree or five\nto one;\nratios\nof ten to one should be avoided\n(Figure\n2-45).\nThe\nextent to which\nexcessive\nbrightness\ncontrast\nis diminished\nis largely\ndetermined\nby the degree\nto which the lighting is indirect,\nthat is, reflected\nfrom\nall directions.\nWhen\nlight is properly\ndiffused there are no shadows,\nno dark corners,\nand no areas\nof relatively high brightness.\nLight fixtures\nin the field of vision should have a surface\nbrightness\nof not over Z candles\nper square\ninch and preferably\nless (412).\nThus,\nvision is generally\nbest\nwhen\nthe surround\nis at the same\nbrightness\nas the central field unless\nthe\nvisual object approaches\nthe thresholds\nof acuity or discrimination,\nin\nwhich\ncase,\nsupplementary\nlighting can be utilized to increase\nthe brightness\nratio, provided\nthe latter does not exceed\nthree or perhaps\nfive to one (271).\nIntensity and distribution,\nhowever,\nmust\nbe coordinated.\nTo increase\nintensity without distribution\nwill only make\na bad situation worse.\nIn fact,\nwhen\ndistribution\nis poor,\nrelatively low intensities\nmust\nbe employed\nto\navoid visual discomfort.\n"a\n\nIn relation to illumination,\ncolor serves\ntwo purposes\nin the perceived\nenvironment.\nIt determines\nthe reflectance\nof colored\nobjects in that\nenvironment,\nand it has a psychosocial\ninfluence\non the emotional\nset of\nindividuals\nin that environment.\nFrom\nthe point of view of visual perception,\nthe reflection factor of walls,\nceilings,\nand furnishings\nof any living or\nworking\nspace\nis more\nimportant\nthan the color used,\nsince the Movement\nof the pointer up or to the right should\nresult\nfrom\n(1) clockwise\nmovement\nof an associated\nrotary\ncontrol,\nor (2) movement\nupward,\nforward,\nor to the right of an associated\nlever or switch.\nNumerals\nshould\nbe placed\non the side of the graduation\nmarks\naway from\nthe\npointer\nto avoid having\nthe numbers\ncovered\nby the pointer.\nFor curved\nor\narc scales,\nthe numerals\nwill be placed\ninside of the graduation\nmarks\nto\navoid\nconstriction\nof the scale.\nIf space is not limited,\nthe numbers\nmay\nbe\nplaced\noutside of the marks\nto avoid having\nthe numbers\ncovered\nby the pointer.\nThe\npointer is located to the right of vertical\nscales and at the bottom\nof\nhorizontal\nscales.\nFixed-pointer,\nmoving-scale\nindicators\nare not recommended\nfor general\nuse (423).\nIf, however,\nthe situation dictates their use, the progression\nof\nnumbers\nand response\nto control\nshould\nfollow those recommended\nfor\nmoving-pointer\nsystems.\nThe numbers\non the indicator\nshould progress\nin\nmagnitude\nin a clockwise\ndirection\naround\nthe dial face.\nIn case of vertical\nor horizontal\nmoving\nstraight scales,\nnumbers\nshould increase\nfrom\nbottom\nto top or from\nleft to right.\nIf the associated\ncontrol has a direct effect on\nthe behavior\nof the vehicle,\nthe scale should\nrotate counter-clockwise\nwith\n(i) clockwise\nmovement\nof the associated\nknob,\nwheel,\nor crank;\n(Z)movernent forward,\nupward,\nor to the right of a lever; or (3) movement\nforward,\nupward,\nor to the right of the vehicle\nor component.\nHowever,\nif the\nassociated\ncontrol has no direct effect on the behavior\nof the vehicle,\nthe\nscale should\nrotate counter-clockwise\nwith counter-clockwise\nmovement\nof\nthe associated\nknob or crank.\nThe pointer\nof lubber\nline position\nshould be\nat 12 o\'clock\nfor right-left directional\ninformation\nand at 9 o\'clock for updown\ninformation.\nFor purely quantitative\ninformation,\neither positlon\nmay\nbe used.\nIf the display\nis used for setting, such as tuning in a desired\nwavelength,\nit is usually advisable\nto cover\nthe unused\nportion\nof the dial\nface.\nThe open window\nshould be large enough\nto permit\nat least one\nnumbered\ngraduation\nto appear\nat each side of any setting.\nThe desirable\nsize of numerals\nand letters is affected by the distance\nat which\nthey are to be read.\nFor the usual reading\ndistance\nof about Z8 in.,\nit has been reported\nthat two different sizes of block capital letters seem\nto\nsatisfy the concurrent\ndesirability\nfor uniform\nsize with occasional\nlarger\nletters for emphasis\n(74). These\ntwo sizes are 9/64 in. for the bulk of the\nletters and II/64 in size for emphasis.\nIllumination,\nreading\nconditions,\ndistance,\nand the importance\nof accuracy\nshould\nof course\nbe taken into\naccount\nin selecting the size of letters or numerals\nfor use as labels or\nmarkings\n(280, 304).\nA formula\nhas been developed\nthat takes into account\nreading\nconditions,\nviewing\ndistance,\nand the importance\naccuracy\n(280, 340):\nH\n\n(height\n\nof letter\n\nin inches)\n\n=\n\n0.00ZZ\n\nD\n\n+\n\nillumination,\nof reading\n\nK 1 +\n\nI<2\n\n(13)\nr\n\nwhere\n\nD\n\n= viewing\n\nK I =\n\ndistance\n\ncorrection\ncondition\n\nfactor\n\nfor illumination\n\nand\n\nviewing\n\n2-85\n\nr\n\n,\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n,J\n\n.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.\n\nK 2\n\ncorrection\nfor importance\nsuch as emergency\nlabels,\nother\n\nconditions,\n\nI4 2\n\n=\n\n(for important\nK 2 = 0.075;\n\nitems\nfor all\n\n0.0).\n\nThis formula\nhas been applied to various\nviewing\ndistances,\nin comblnation with the other variables,\nand the heights\nof letters and n\' merals\nderived\ntherefrom.\nThese\nvalues\nare given in Table\n2-63.\nIt should be kept in mind\nthat these are approximations\nof desirable\nheights; values\nwithin reason\nof\nthose given would\ngenerally\nproduce\nrelatively comparable\nlegibility.\nNeedless to say, one should\nnot apply such a formula\narbitrarily,\nwithout taking\ninto account\nspecial facets of the particular\nsituation.\nA set of recommended\nheights\nfor the Apollo\nSystem\nat 28" viewing\ndistance,\nlow brightness\n(down\nto0.03\nftL) range from\n0.05 to 0. At night\nappear\nas a moonlit\nobject against the dark night sky or the surface\ndark Earth.\n\nr\n\n.\n\n.\n\nThe adaptation\nof the astronaut\nis determined\nby the average\nluminance\nof the illumination\nincreases\nwhen\nthe window\nfaces the sun or the sunlit Earth.\nOn the other hand,\ndark\n\n-\n\nadaptatlon\nis a slow process\nrequiring\nat least 15-30 minutes\n(depending\non\nprevious\nconditions)\nto achieve\na fairly stationary\nhigh sensitivity\n(Figure\n2-4Z and Z-43).\nIn orbit, a terminal\ndark adaptation\nmay\nbe difficult to\nobtain in view about !\nto 2 m. away, one becomes\neffectively\nmyopic by this amount.\nEmpty visual\nfield myopia\ncan increase\nthe light threshold\nby a factor of log 0.3 or double\nthe minimum\nvisual\nangle (329, _8).\nIt is difficult\nto say how serious\na\nfactor empty visual\nfield myopia would be in space flights\nsince an astronaut\nis not constantly\nscanning\na homogeneous\nfield.\nWhen operations.\n\n.-.\n\n.\n:\n\n7\n\n.\n.\n\n"\n\n.-.\n\n- ir\n\n,\n\nIn the present\ncontext,\nthese factors\nare interrelated\nin a complex\nway.\nUniform\nsensitivity\nexists over the retina at the parafovea\nand then a more or less leveling\noff.\nThe fovea is blind for targets\nof low\nluminances.\nValues\ndetermined\nfor parafoveal\nvision\nare also nearly\ncorrect\nfor a more Figures\n2-2L and\n\n"\n\n..\n\n--\n\n,\n\n"t\n\n"\n\n2-?_).\nThe findings\non stationary\ntargets\nmay only be applicable\nin space when\nboth satellites\nmove with the same speed and in the same I stellar magnitude\nbrighter\nthan for detection\nof a\nstationary\ntarget on a starless\nbackground.\nThe difference\nincreased\nrapidly\nas vision began to change over to cone or photopic\nvision.\nA moving\ntarget\nstimulates\nnot just one point on the retina\nbut successive\nretinal\nregions.\nIn scotopic\nvision,\nsuccessively\nstimulated\nretinal receptors\nmay be\nof equal or similar\nsensitivity,\nwhereas,\nin photopic\nvision\nthe image of the\ntarget will stimulate\nless sensitive\nareas as soon as it moves from the fovea.\nThus, under these as\n\nyet clear.\n\nIn space\noperations,\nthe estimation\nof the distance\nof another\nspaceship is of crucial\nimportance.\nThe recognition\nof the distance\nis a complex\nfunction which\ninvolves\nthe integration\nof immediate\nvisual impresslons\nand\nprevious\nexperience\n(4, 179, 180, 182).\nFor a moving\nlight point far out in\nspace,\nmany\ncues of depth perception\nare terrain.\nIn case a terrain is lacking,\ndistance\njudgment\nbecomes\nunreliable and, without\nsize cues,\nonly relative distances\nare recognized,\nnamely\nthat one object is nearer\nthan the other.\nAn important\nempirical\ndistance\nfactor is the apparent\nsize.\nIn order\nfor this factor to give information about the distance\nof an object in a homogeneous\nsurround,\nthe real\nsize of the object must\nbe known.\nWhen\nthe actual size of the target is\nunknown,\nits distance\nwill be indeterminate,\nsince the same\nangular\nsize\ncan correspond\nto a small object nearby\nor a large object at great distance\n(See Figure\n2-74).\n\nor\n\nOne secondary\ndistance\nfactor is motion\nthe gradual\nchange\nin the rate of apparent\n\nperspective\ndisplacement\n\nor motion\nparallax\nof The use of these cues in\nnight operations is another problem which needs further study (417).\n.\n\nSince the sky above the horizon will appear essentially black (unless the\nSun or Earth is in the field of view) the adaptive capabilities of the man on\nthe lunar surface may be taxed and his visual performance under such conditions cannot confidently be predicted from existing data (410). For the\nsunlit and earthlit conditions, the highly directional nature of the illumination\nwill, especially at low angles, combine with the low average surface reflectance to produce wide extremes in the appearance of the terrain (IIZ)\n(Figure Z-I l). injured\ncells\nat relatively\nslow rates.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.\n\nr I\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n_\n\nr\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFigure\nZ-79 shows\nan action spectrum\nfor the erythema\nof sunburn\nof\nhuman\nskin obtained\nby determinlng,\nfor a range of wavelengths,\nthe dose of\nradiation\nrequired\nto elicit a just perceptible\nreddening.\nNumerous\nfactors\ncontribute\nto the complexity\nof the erythema\nresponse,\nand each may\nhave\nsomewhat\ndifferent action spectra.\nThis would help to explain the apparent\nparadox\nthat reciprocity\n(dose-rate\nx time = constant) holds very well when\nerythema\nproduction\nis studied with monochromatic\nradiation,\nwhereas\nwith\npolychromatic\nradiation this is not the case (62).\nThe erythema\nspectrum\nhas sometimes\nbeen used as a standard\nfor comparison\nof the action of sunlight with the action of artificial sources;\nbut it is clear that with these\ncomplicating\nfactors there may\nbe considerable\ninaccuracy\ninvolved.\nThickening\n\nof Skin\n\nA result of the action of ultraviolet\nradiation\non the epidermis\nis a rapid\nproliferation\nof the cells of the Malpighian\nlayer.\nThis leads to thickening\nof that layer and also of the corneum,\nas many\nof the Malpighian\ncells die\nand are incorporated\ninto the horny\nlayer (60).\nThickening\nof the corneum\nresults in a marked\ndecrease\nin the amount\nof sunburn-producing\nradiation\nthat reaches\nthe Malpighian\nlayer; that is, the effectiveness\nof the corneum\nas a filter is increased,\nwith a resultant\nreduction\nin sensitivity to sunburn.\nIn white-skinned\npeople,\nthe thickening\nof the corneum\nis clearly a principal\ncause\nof the apparent\nimmunity\nto sunburn\nthat is experienced\nafter exposure\nto sunlight or other source\nof ultraviolet\nradiation.\nThis point is not generally\nunderstood,\nand it is commonly\nthought\nthat the tanning\nof the skin, that is,\nthe production\nof melanin\npigment\nin the epidermis,\nis the only thing that\ngives protection\nfrom\nsunburn.\n\n_\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nr .\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nr.,\n\nr\n\nTanning\nThe tanning\nprocess\nis itself quite complicated.\nIn white races the\nmelanin\npigment,\nwhich confers\nthe tan color,\nis found principally\nin the\ndeepest\npart of the Malpighian\nlayer (60, 62, g14, 215).\nAbout\ntwenty\nhours\nafter the exposure\nto ultraviolet\nlight, some\nof this melanin\nbegins\nto\nmigrate\ntoward\nthe surface,\nand following\nthis there is production\nof new\npigment\nin the basal layer.\nLater,\nwith the death of cells from\nthe Malpighian\nlayer and their incorporation\ninto the corneum,\nthe melanin\ngets into that\nlayer too.\nIn Negro\nskin the melanin\nis present\nin greater\nquantity\nand is also\ndistributed\nmore\nuniformly\nthrough\nthe epidermis,\nincluding\nthe corneum.\nNegro\nskin not only contains\nmore\nmelanin\nbut also is thicker\n(411), both\nfactors apparently\ncontributing\nto the greater\nopacity that is associated\nwith\nlow susceptibility\nto sunburn\n(60).\nUltimately,\nif the skin is not exposed,\nthe\nmelanin\nnot only bleaches\nbut also may\ndisappear\ncompletely;\nbut the\nZ-l14\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nI\n\nk\n_\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2_ ;\n\ni\n\n. i i .....\n_\n\n\':-:\n\n;\n\n:\n\n:\n\ndisappearance\nmay\ntake months\nor even produced\nby\nultraviolet\nbut by focusing\nof light in the visible spectrum\non the retinal\nstructures\n(See Section A - Visible\nLight).\nClaims\nthat ultraviolet\nrays may\ndamage\nthe retina seem\nunfounded.\nIt is doubtful that either the ultraviolet\nor the total energy\nfrom\nsunlight can cause\ncataract\nin human\neyes, that is,\nopacification\nof the lens.\nThis may\nbe brought\nabout,\nhowever,\nby radiation\nfrom\nlower\ntemperature\nsources,\nsuch as glass blowers\'\nfurnaces\n(glass\nblowers\'\ncataract),\nwhich\nmay\nhave much\nmore\nemission\nin the infrared\nthan\n\ndoes\n\nsunlight\n\nEye\n\nProtection\n\n(60).\n\nAgainst\n\nL\n\nUltraviolet\n\nradiation\nis reflected\nat each glass air surface\nwhen\nthe radiation is incident\nnormal\nto the surface.\nAnother\n4 percent\nis reflected at the surface\nwhere\nthe light emerges,\nso that even if the glass had no absorption\n(and consequentl_\n100 percent\ninternal transmittance),its\nexternal\ntransmittance\nwould\nonly be\n92 pe rc ent.\nA convenient\nway of presenting\ninformation\nabout the internal transmittance\nof substances,\nI/I<), is to give the values\nof the absorption\ncoefficient\nat various\nwavelengths.\nKnowing\nthis, the transmittance\nfor any particular\nthickness\nmay\nbe calculated\nfrom\nEquation\n2-20.\nFor convenience\nin calculating, this relation is given graphically\nin Figure\n2-82a where\nvalues\nof\ntransmittance\nare plotted as a function of a with the thickness\nof the absorbing medium\nas a parameter.\nFigure\n2-8Zb\nshows\nthe values\nof transmittance\nas a function\nof the thickness\nof the medium\nwith absorption\ncoefficient\nas a\nparameter.\nData are available\non the absorption\ncoefficient of many\ndifferent glasses\nand plastics\n(Z50).\nThe transmission\nof glass in the ultraviolet\nis determined\nlargely by the iron oxide content\n(ferric state) which\nabsorbs\nstrongly\nin this region.\nImpurities\nof less than 0.01 percent\nmay\nhave profound\neffects.\nFigure\n2-81 indicates\nthe absorption\ncharacteristics\nof the common\nglasses.\nA 2mm.\nthickness\nof crown\nglass reduces\nthe exposure\nhazard\napproximately\nfifteenfold at 305 millimicrons,\nforty-five fold at 297 millimicrons\nand a hundred\nfold at Z68 millimicrons\n(320).\nA
